  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer10553440245375248856">Escrita en 1952 pero no publicada hasta 1985 debido a su franca plasmación del deseo homosexual, "Queer", obra temprana de William S. Burroughs, es al mismo tiempo un descarnado autorretrato narrativo, una historia de amor brutalmente realista, una grotesca fantasía tragicómica y una ingeniosa novela política. Un libro que proporciona muchas claves fundamentales para adent</span>
  <span id="freeText10553440245375248856" style="display:none">Escrita en 1952 pero no publicada hasta 1985 debido a su franca plasmación del deseo homosexual, "Queer", obra temprana de William S. Burroughs, es al mismo tiempo un descarnado autorretrato narrativo, una historia de amor brutalmente realista, una grotesca fantasía tragicómica y una ingeniosa novela política. Un libro que proporciona muchas claves fundamentales para adentrarse en el arrollador universo literario del autor. Esta edición definitiva, editada con motivo del 25 aniversario de su primera publicación, incorpora una extensa y documentada introducción de Oliver Harris en la que se repasan las complejas circunstancias personales en las que la obra fue escrita y que marcarían la vida y la posterior trayectoria literaria de Burroughs. Y se incluye también, a modo de epílogo, el texto que el propio autor escribió como prólogo para la edición de 1985.<br /><br />"Queer" está ambientada en un inmenso suburbio, que Burroughs definiría más tarde como la «Interzona», y que abarca desde la Ciudad de México, capital mundial del delito, hasta Panamá. Un álter ego del escritor, Lee, teje su tela amorosa en torno a Allerton, un joven ambiguo, indiferente como un animal. Deambula por locales cada vez más sórdidos, en los que pulula una fauna en estado de descomposición, y en esas excursiones, como un pícaro alienado, nos regala astillas radiactivas de su negrísimo humor. Para resolver sus obsesiones mortíferas y sexuales, Lee parte con su amigo a la búsqueda de la ayahuasca, droga absoluta capaz de otorgar el control total sobre los cerebros, y por eso mismo codiciada por Rusia y Estados Unidos... y por todo adicto. Dispuesto a abismarse en todos los peligros, como un santo o un criminal con orden de búsqueda y captura, Lee no tiene nada que perder. En esta novela aflora por primera vez ese paisaje alucinado que hoy todo lector reconoce como el mundo particular de William S. Burroughs.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10553440245375248856" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/123274031?book_show_action=true">Sep 24, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer123274031" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6425702971434363279">I have a passionate hatred for William Burroughs. I think even his fans have to concede that he's a degenerate piece of shit. I admit my prior experience with him consists of 5 pages of Naked Lunch and a couple biographies of various sorts, none of which fail to mention the pedophilia and him murdering his wife (I'm from Detroit, don't think for a second I buy his bullshit story), not that I'd hold that against him when rating this book. <br/> <br/>I went into this book expecting it to be about heroin abu</span>
  <span id="freeText6425702971434363279" style="display:none">I have a passionate hatred for William Burroughs. I think even his fans have to concede that he's a degenerate piece of shit. I admit my prior experience with him consists of 5 pages of Naked Lunch and a couple biographies of various sorts, none of which fail to mention the pedophilia and him murdering his wife (I'm from Detroit, don't think for a second I buy his bullshit story), not that I'd hold that against him when rating this book. <br /> <br />I went into this book expecting it to be about heroin abuse and gay sex, you know some fun light reading, instead it turns about to be a sober, heartbreaking tale of profound universal human loneliness: <i>"In deep sadness there is no place for sentimentality. It is as the mountains: a fact. There it is. When you realize it, you cannot complain."</i> <br /> <br />The fact that this book is great somehow only makes me hate him more. <br /> <br /><br /><br /><br /><br />---------------------------------------------------------------------<br />Alright Burroughs, I don't like you and you don't like me, but I found this for .50 cents today so I'm giving you one more shot to redeem yourself . . . Don't Fuck This Up!</span>
  <a data-text-id="6425702971434363279" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer31313463" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7063835541264716046">Here's the thing that puzzles me about this book: why was it not published until 1985 while the far, far more offensive <i>Naked Lunch</i> was published (<a href='http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naked_Lunch#Literary_significance_and_reception' rel='nofollow noopener'>not without obstacles</a>) in 1959? One idea is that Burroughs put the manuscript for <i>Queer</i> away for many years and chose not to revisit it because it reminded him of a extremely terrible time in his life, the time surrounding the well-known (<a href='http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0211941/' rel='nofollow noopener'>and unfortunately adapted to the screen</a>) accidental killing of his wife during a drunken game of William Tell (a "</span>
  <span id="freeText7063835541264716046" style="display:none">Here's the thing that puzzles me about this book: why was it not published until 1985 while the far, far more offensive <i>Naked Lunch</i> was published (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naked_Lunch#Literary_significance_and_reception" rel="nofollow noopener">not without obstacles</a>) in 1959? One idea is that Burroughs put the manuscript for <i>Queer</i> away for many years and chose not to revisit it because it reminded him of a extremely terrible time in his life, the time surrounding the well-known (<a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0211941/" rel="nofollow noopener">and unfortunately adapted to the screen</a>) accidental killing of his wife during a drunken game of William Tell (a "parlor trick" which primarily involves shooting objects off of a person's head with a gun). He goes into some detail about this emotional difficulty in the fairly remarkable introduction written in the aforementioned year of the book’s long overdue publication. <br /><br />There isn’t a single description in this book that could be considered sexually explicit, homosexual or otherwise. Apparently even the slightest allusion to homosexuality while failing to regard it as a immoral, objectionable or otherwise contemptible aspect of existence was enough to prevent the book from being published, yet, <i>Naked Lunch</i> is immeasurably more graphic and unsettling in every dimension than <i>Queer</i>. So it seems that this book must have gone unpublished for 30 plus years solely due to Burroughs need to keep it hidden from <i>himself</i>.<br /><br />So the book is a sequel to Burroughs’ novelistic debut <i>Junky</i> and both are quite autobiographical. To put it simply Lee <i>is</i> William S. Burroughs. According to some brief research Eugene Allerton is also based on a real person: <br /><br />"Adelbert Lewis Marker (1930-1998), a recently discharged American Navy serviceman from Jacksonville, Florida who befriended Burroughs in Mexico City" (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Queer_(novel)#Plot_summary" rel="nofollow noopener">source</a>). This can be affirmed through a read of <i>Letters to Allen Ginsberg, 1953-1957</i>.<br /><br />The plot is rather simple. Lee is trying to kick a heroin habit while on the lam in Mexico City. He’s avoiding Stateside drug charges until the 5 year statute of limitations is reached. He’s wandering around, drinking alcohol constantly and looking for some properly satisfying sex with men, specifically his eyes latch onto Eugene Allerton. As a brief aside here, let me say that some of the very concise descriptions of extreme lust are really well done. Though I don't identify with the specific objectives of Burroughs' lust (i.e. wishing to be "had" by and to "have" a flock of adolescent Ecuadorian boys in a muddy riverbed, etc) nonetheless lust is lust is lust is lust. Ok, so this Allerton fella is sort of queer--"obliging" is how Burroughs puts it. He's willing to have sex with men but not all that into it. That sorta thing. Their relationship doesn’t stray much from this basic dynamic. <br /><br />Eventually Lee sets up a sex-for-money deal which includes (in addition to sexual demands being met at least twice a week) that Allerton is to accompany him on trip to Ecuador in search of "Yage" (a.k.a. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ayahuasca" rel="nofollow noopener">ayahuasca</a>), a plant Lee believes to possess telepathy-inducing properties. He believes that the Russians and Americans are trying to extract these telepathic powers from the plant for obvious, ethically dubious, authoritarian intentions (i.e. mind-control <i>en masse</i>). However all Lee wants it for is so that he can make pretty boys in the street and Allerton himself his sexual slaves. So he no longer has to deal with all of the socio-psychological annoyances that come with being a sex seeking gay male in the 1950’s. Lee talking to Allerton:<br /><br /><i>"While we are in Ecuador we must score for Yage," Lee said. "Think of it: thought control. Take anyone apart and rebuild to your taste. Anything about somebody bugs you, you say, 'Yage! I want that routine took clear out of his mind.' I could think of a few changes I might make in you, doll." He looked at Allerton and licked his lips. "You'd be so much</i> nicer <i> after a few alterations. You're nice now but you do have those irritating peculiarities. I mean, you won't do exactly what I want you to do all the time."</i> <br /><br />I enjoyed the book for three basic reasons (in no particular order):<br /><br />1) The descriptions of Mexico City and various towns within Ecuador during the late 40’s/early 50’s for their cultural and historical value.<br /><br />2) The expression of what kind of psychological pains faced a homosexual in the not so distant past due to the obvious massive intolerance and condemnation of such people. The word "queer" began as a pejorative term for homosexuals. Burroughs is writing during a time when this was still the case, before people had reappropriated the term and also before the word "gay" was also reappropriated to put a more positive spin on what it means to be a homosexual. I found this basic reason for enjoying the book to also bring about the only real feelings of empathy for cranky and often cruel and morbid ol’ Lee. <br /><br /><i>He felt a killing hate for the stupid, ordinary, disapproving people who kept him from doing what he wanted to do. "Someday I am going to have things just like I want," he said to himself. "And if any moralizing son of a bitch gives me any static, they will fish him out of the river."</i><br /><br />It got me thinking about how truly unfortunate it is that homosexuals were—and still are in many socio-cultural contexts—forced to hide their sexuality, to develop the most ugly of self-conceptions, to fear for their lives all due to a hatred and prejudice that has no defensible, rational basis whatsoever. On a lighter note, it also made me feel an appreciation for the progress that has been made throughout the world to combat this irrationally-based prejudice. Though gays are still executed <i>simply for being gay</i> in Iran and other nations, in most western countries things have improved greatly for homosexuals.<br /><br />3) Identifying the seedlings of what would eventually become the style of "classic Burroughs." Most of the novel is fairly straight ahead third-person narrative but a few bizarre <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7437.Naked_Lunch_The_Restored_Text" title="Naked Lunch The Restored Text by William S. Burroughs" rel="nofollow noopener">Naked Lunch</a>
</i>-like descriptions flash upon the page once and a while, mostly during what are known as Lee’s "routines." These "routines" are Lee’s oft-intoxicated rambling bar-side orations. For example we come to this monologue (voiced to no one in particular) in the midst of fairly normal third-person description of Lee shadowing his current sexual interest Eugene Allerton who is leaving the bar with a woman named Mary:<br /><br /><i>"Sometimes he</i> [an Italian chess master] <i>used smoke screens to hide his maneuvers from the opposition—I mean literal smoke screens, of course. He had corps of trained idiots who would rush in at a given signal and eat all the pieces. With defeat staring him in the face—as it often did, because actually he knew nothing of chess but the rules and wasn’t too sure of those—he would leap up yelling, 'You cheap bastard! I saw you palm that queen!' and ram a broken teacup into his opponent’s face. In 1922 he was rid out of Prague on a rail. The next time I saw Tetrazzini</i> [the "Italian chess master":] <i>was in Upper Ubangi. A complete wreck. Peddling unlicensed condoms. That was the year of rinderpest, when everything died, even the hyenas."</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="7063835541264716046" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7210211343527973498">Certain “cult” writing earns this status because the prose is so transparent and simple it instantly appeals to teenage males done with Easton Ellis and Kerouac who want to up their shock quotient before attempting to read <i>Gravity’s Rainbow</i> for the first and last time. <i>Queer</i> fits the bill except, by today’s standards, the book is a little prude in tight Speedos with its danglies between its thighs asking us to love it if we’d only give it a chance. Will Lee is a homosexual-in-training in pursuit</span>
  <span id="freeText7210211343527973498" style="display:none">Certain “cult” writing earns this status because the prose is so transparent and simple it instantly appeals to teenage males done with Easton Ellis and Kerouac who want to up their shock quotient before attempting to read <i>Gravity’s Rainbow</i> for the first and last time. <i>Queer</i> fits the bill except, by today’s standards, the book is a little prude in tight Speedos with its danglies between its thighs asking us to love it if we’d only give it a chance. Will Lee is a homosexual-in-training in pursuit of reluctant, disobliging ass that often makes him cry, so unsure is he of his own sexuality. This is a weird piece of tortuousness. But an interesting one.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15171746790596310398">seriously, Lee, will you give it a rest? stop trying to get into the pants of that straight guy. get some dignity. try getting into the pants of some dignity! Lee, i hate to tell you this, but you are embarrassing yourself. you're desperate and that is highly unattractive. even worse, you surround yourself with the same decay that is present in your decayed view of the world. and when that isn't enough, you seek out even more decay, until the novel becomes a travelogue of depressing decay, decay</span>
  <span id="freeText15171746790596310398" style="display:none">seriously, Lee, will you give it a rest? stop trying to get into the pants of that straight guy. get some dignity. try getting into the pants of some dignity! Lee, i hate to tell you this, but you are embarrassing yourself. you're desperate and that is highly unattractive. even worse, you surround yourself with the same decay that is present in your decayed view of the world. and when that isn't enough, you seek out even more decay, until the novel becomes a travelogue of depressing decay, decay, decay. all the while trying pathetically to suck the dick of some serviceman who actually is neither your friend nor queer. you give queers a bad name! and so i resent even the title of this novel of your boring misadventures. you are as bad as some drunk straight guy at a party who is so wasted that he doesn't even know that the girl he is hitting on so sloppily is rolling her eyes at him in boredom and disgust. No Means No!<br /><br />however, Queer, you do get 1 extra star because you may serve as a somewhat easy entry point into the writing of william s. burroughs, who went on to write so much that was greater. and, well, the writing in this one isn't really bad at all. it is the story that revolts; the writing is on the interesting side of really-not-bad-at-all.</span>

<div id="review_1228487649" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1228487649" />
    <a title="Joey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10049704-joey"><img alt="Joey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1493309397p2/10049704.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1228487649?book_show_action=true">Mar 15, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joey" href="/user/show/10049704-joey">Joey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143117834" href="/book/show/7958210-queer">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10049704-joey?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10049704-joey?shelf=fictions">fictions</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1228487649" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1492543425208247566">A conservative reader might ask his Goodreads friends , “ Is William Burroughs gay? “ as if Burrough knows how it feels like being queer, an offensive slang for homosexual. If he is, it is neither here nor there because he is able to depict the reality of the homosexual world , categorically, the desire to establish an intimate relationship with a straight guy. So if you are gay, you might be able to empathize the situation of the protagonist. But if you are a straight guy, you might end up real</span>
  <span id="freeText1492543425208247566" style="display:none">A conservative reader might ask his Goodreads friends , “ Is William Burroughs gay? “ as if Burrough knows how it feels like being queer, an offensive slang for homosexual. If he is, it is neither here nor there because he is able to depict the reality of the homosexual world , categorically, the desire to establish an intimate relationship with a straight guy. So if you are gay, you might be able to empathize the situation of the protagonist. But if you are a straight guy, you might end up realizing that not all gays are “ lecherously fellatioes” by nature. They are not far different from “coquettishly fellatrixes” .(laughs )<br /><br /><br />The book depicts the life of the queer , particularly how they get into an intimate relationship with the same sex. In the story, although not quite clear , the protagonist, Lee wants to build an intimate relationship with Allerton. The story is not clear if he is a straight guy, but the story says that he does not like to make friends with a queer. Eventually, Lee and Allerton becomes friends since Lee is determined to get along with him. They spend time together in the same bed. Again , the story is not quite clear if they make love, for Allerton does not want Lee to keep on touching him. In effect, Lee has secret sexual desire for Allerton; however, he has fear to get in on the act, for Allerton appears to be a straight guy. <br /><br /> If you are a queer, you may be able to empathize with the deeper side of Lee. But admit it. This is the fact to some extent that queers’ real intention whenever they make friends with a guy is to build an intimate relationship. No wonder that the common perception of guys is that queers are cock-suckers. (laughs) But not all queers have this kind of sexual behavior pattern. As a matter of fact, such queers are not far different from coquettish women. People are not just used to this kind of sexual human evolution. <br /><br />In the Philippines, queers appear to be divided into four categories : they are closet gays, open gays, transvestites, and the transgendered. Closet gays are known as peppermint or paminta in Filipino because they are bound to get pulverized to come out in the open. Open gays appear to be naïve , but still they are known for their effeminate mannerisms unlike transvestites and the transgendered for their flamboyantly feministic fashion. The latter three kinds of gays are likely to be socially accepted because they are more open to their self-identities (despite the fact that the atmosphere is not yet completely conducive. )However, peppermints have a hard time; consequently, they face different dilemmas. <br /><br />It is my first William Burroughs’s novel. Whenever I thumbed it through on my list, I always wondered why its title is Queer since its paperback picture looks immaterial. When I got the chance to read it, I got Burroughs’s method to his madness: internalizing queers’ feelings.<br />For this reason, impressed by his unique twist of writing, I heard that his critically acclaimed NAKED LUNCH is a good read. I will buy it at any cost as soon as I spot it. ^^<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1492543425208247566" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText15339302809491528023" style="display:none">Lee, Chapter 4: "Got an idea for a new dish. Or, if we run a dramatic joint, a screaming pig covered with burning brandy rushes out of the kitchen and dies right by your chair. Light, touching, funny, yet dangerous, <i>Queer</i> isn't cuffed by the terse factual allegiance of <i>Junky</i> or the unchecked typhoon of spazz that makes <i>

</i> a slight disappointment. Instead we have sympathy for the devil, cringing for the maladroit, and a fascination for a man who was neither social butterfly nor parasite, but a bizarre insect somewhere in the middle.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3058314930205867513">I tried. I effing tried.<br/><br/>I can appreciate the book for what it is. Publishing (even writing) a queer-themed book was daring and subversive in that era. Living that life was dangerous. So in that respect, I can appreciate it for breaking ground, etc. <br/><br/>That said, I hated the book. Or I should say, I hated the half of the book I managed to get through before I finally gave up because...I hated it.   <span id="freeText3058314930205867513" style="display:none">I tried. I effing tried.<br /><br />I can appreciate the book for what it is. Publishing (even writing) a queer-themed book was daring and subversive in that era. Living that life was dangerous. So in that respect, I can appreciate it for breaking ground, etc. <br /><br />That said, I hated the book. Or I should say, I hated the half of the book I managed to get through before I finally gave up because...I hated it. Lee is obnoxious, judgmental, entitled, and at times downright creepy in his pursuit of Eugene. I don't expect characters to be sympathetic all the time (in fact, I rather enjoy anti-heroes), but for God's sake, they need to be INTERESTING. Lee was about as fascinating as a teenager fixating on the rock star they're determined to marry, regardless of what that rock star thinks of the idea.<br /><br />DNF.</span>

Then I came across this thin novel at a used bookstore and figured this was a good place to start. Not only did I enjoy the novel, I loved that Burroughs had written the introduction. Queer was written in 1949, but wasn't published until 1985. Burroughs explains the reasons behind this and other info. that was fascinating. I'm a </span>
  <span id="freeText18149527894793805352" style="display:none">I have wanted to read Burroughs for a while and was going to start with Naked Lunch, but I kept hearing mixed opinions, so I continued to push it to the back of my TBR. Then I came across this thin novel at a used bookstore and figured this was a good place to start. Not only did I enjoy the novel, I loved that Burroughs had written the introduction. Queer was written in 1949, but wasn't published until 1985. Burroughs explains the reasons behind this and other info. that was fascinating. I'm a big fan of the style of writing where it's fiction but you know the author is writing about themselves and their experiences. So this novel was right in my wheelhouse. Can't wait to read more of Burroughs' work. </span>

<div id="review_3327942312" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3327942312" />
    <a title="George" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10168276-george"><img alt="George" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1511867378p2/10168276.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3327942312?book_show_action=true">May 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="George" class="user" itemprop="url" name="George" href="/user/show/10168276-george">George</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10168276-george?shelf=1001-books-list-read-2006">1001-books-list-read-2006</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3327942312" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4254235020902784627">A short novella mainly about unrequited love and loneliness. William Lee, a homosexual drug addict spends his days in a number of bars and gay joints. He becomes interested in Eugene Allerton, who shows passing interest in Lee. Lee persuades Eugene to travel with him to South America in search of yage, a drug with supposedly telepathic qualities. Written in 1952 and first published in 1985, the novella is semi autobiographical. <br/><br/>Readers new to William Burroughs should firstly read 'Junky' and 'Th</span>
  <span id="freeText4254235020902784627" style="display:none">A short novella mainly about unrequited love and loneliness. William Lee, a homosexual drug addict spends his days in a number of bars and gay joints. He becomes interested in Eugene Allerton, who shows passing interest in Lee. Lee persuades Eugene to travel with him to South America in search of yage, a drug with supposedly telepathic qualities. Written in 1952 and first published in 1985, the novella is semi autobiographical. <br /><br />Readers new to William Burroughs should firstly read 'Junky' and 'The Naked Lunch'. A very good, highly recommended novel on a gay lovers relationship is James Baldwin's 'Giovanni's Room'.<br /><br />Here are some examples of the author's writing style:<br />'In deep sadness there is no place for sentimentality. It is as final as the mountains; a fact. There it is. When you realize it you cannot complain.'<br />'Like many people who have nothing to do, he was very resentful of any claims on his time.'<br />'Anything that can be accomplished chemically can be accomplished in other ways.'</span>

<div id="review_1265079243" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1265079243" />
    <a title="Amy (folkpants)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/225722-amy-folkpants"><img alt="Amy (folkpants)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1450827225p2/225722.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1265079243?book_show_action=true">Apr 26, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amy (folkpants)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amy (folkpants)" href="/user/show/225722-amy-folkpants">Amy (folkpants)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1265079243" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9442855719772212410">This is my first time reading Burroughs even though I've been meaning to for years. I admit that the generation of writers that he was a part of- the beat generation- has never greatly appealed to me. I can't really relate to being on the road and high all the time. But, at the same time, it does lead to great story telling. Queer had a bit of that crazy on the road feel. But, for me, it also had a ton of very intimate, private emotions that I could relate to. Lee's desperation for any kind of a</span>
  <span id="freeText9442855719772212410" style="display:none">This is my first time reading Burroughs even though I've been meaning to for years. I admit that the generation of writers that he was a part of- the beat generation- has never greatly appealed to me. I can't really relate to being on the road and high all the time. But, at the same time, it does lead to great story telling. Queer had a bit of that crazy on the road feel. But, for me, it also had a ton of very intimate, private emotions that I could relate to. Lee's desperation for any kind of attention from the people in his life and then, specifically, Allerton was heart-wrenching. The overwhelming desire just to be touched and recognized. Begging for Allerton to be intimate with him. Realizing there is something very queer about himself (all meanings of the word) but still needing so badly to fill a void. Realizing that he may actually be a bit of a joke in the eyes of Allerton, but still so very very desperate to hold on to the very little bit of attention and intimacy that Allerton does allow him. He's willing to become a fool. This feeling of desperation and taking any little scraps that are thrown your way are all too familiar. I found the story of Lee so true in many ways and so unbelievably gut wrenching. I realize how willing a person can become (me) to do anything for just a little bit when you feel so alone in the world you live in.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9442855719772212410" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9809050533239333419">It's more like a 3 and a half but it feels wrong to bump this book down to 3. <br/><br/>It's a fascinating read. Not because a lot happens, but because it's impossible to read it without acknowledging that Lee is Burroughs himself. The poor guy is gay and not 100% on terms with it, even though he sleeps with men, and he has a serious drug addiction, that, a third of the way through the book, vanishes as he goes through withdrawal. In some ways it's not a light read. In other ways it is. <br/><br/>This books has Lee</span>
  <span id="freeText9809050533239333419" style="display:none">It's more like a 3 and a half but it feels wrong to bump this book down to 3. <br /><br />It's a fascinating read. Not because a lot happens, but because it's impossible to read it without acknowledging that Lee is Burroughs himself. The poor guy is gay and not 100% on terms with it, even though he sleeps with men, and he has a serious drug addiction, that, a third of the way through the book, vanishes as he goes through withdrawal. In some ways it's not a light read. In other ways it is. <br /><br />This books has Lee along with his companion that he occasionally sleeps with, traveling through Mexico down to South America. That's not what this book is about, even though Lee is lonely and constantly afraid of Allerton rejecting him, and is also in search of a drug he has heard about. Going back to the idea that Lee is Burroughs, then you have to listen to the original introduction. This book, though it avoids it and never brings it up, is about the accidental death of Burroughs' wife, Joan. It's about the pall a death at your hands casts over your life. So while the drugs and the sexuality contribute to what this book is about, neither are the main things. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9672889399534481291">This is pulp fiction at its finest, and the perfect companion to Burroughs better known "Junky." <div id="review_393492559" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/393492559" />
    <a title="Mat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7120830-mat"><img alt="Mat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585499177p2/7120830.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/393492559?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mat" href="/user/show/7120830-mat">Mat</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer393492559" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12809540971035613455">Okay, Okay. I wouldn't go so far as to call myself 'homophobic' but let's just say I feel slightly uncomfortable reading a book with the title of <i>Queer</i> on a crowded train. And I am not talking about any standard commuter train. I am talking about a jam-packed sushi-fest of people that is the Tokyo commuter train. But I realize that it's nonsense to feel that way. Who cares? <br/>I'm a heterosexual man but do have some friends who are homosexual/lesbian and am totally cool being around them. Have no p</span>
  <span id="freeText12809540971035613455" style="display:none">Okay, Okay. I wouldn't go so far as to call myself 'homophobic' but let's just say I feel slightly uncomfortable reading a book with the title of <i>Queer</i> on a crowded train. And I am not talking about any standard commuter train. I am talking about a jam-packed sushi-fest of people that is the Tokyo commuter train. But I realize that it's nonsense to feel that way. Who cares? <br />I'm a heterosexual man but do have some friends who are homosexual/lesbian and am totally cool being around them. Have no problem with what someone's sexuality is whatsoever. That's totally up to them; their business. So what is it about this book that I'm afraid of?<br /><br />Well, it just might turn out to be a big fat 'nothing'. I have only just started reading this and have so far found it to be one truly marvellous novel. The prose is searingly intense and visceral, written in a similar style to <i>Junky</i>. This is written in the same straight-linear narrative as Junky and it ranks up there among his best.<br />It's raw, gripping and Burroughs' pain expressed through the protagonist Lee is so well transfered to the pages that you can almost feel it and cut it with a knife.<br />The pursuit of his lust/love, Allerton, came across as a bit of a vacuous, selfish youth but Lee does not let up in his pursuit and in the end you can see how he sort of 'drove' him away. <br />Great story. For the faint-hearted, this is not a book with graphic sexual detail and grotesque imagery, far from it. Burroughs was such a great writer even at this early stage of his career. The tone is so different from his later novels where it became increasingly more sardonic and sinister.<br />Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12809540971035613455" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/542516337?book_show_action=true">Feb 21, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer542516337" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer10624914050221787602">I really enjoyed this book. "Queer" is interesting to me as you can see Burroughs' evolution as a writer and the novel also has a foreboding quality that many attribute to Burroughs' accidental murder of his common law wife. The actual plot of the book is pretty basic, it involves William Lee's infatuation with a young man in Mexico. The novel is unflinching in it's portrayal of blind lust; Burroughs' character makes a fool of himself on many occasions, but the novel shouldn't just be seen as on</span>
  <span id="freeText10624914050221787602" style="display:none">I really enjoyed this book. "Queer" is interesting to me as you can see Burroughs' evolution as a writer and the novel also has a foreboding quality that many attribute to Burroughs' accidental murder of his common law wife. The actual plot of the book is pretty basic, it involves William Lee's infatuation with a young man in Mexico. The novel is unflinching in it's portrayal of blind lust; Burroughs' character makes a fool of himself on many occasions, but the novel shouldn't just be seen as one mans affection for another, it is so much more than that. It is an examination on the power of control and addiction. The novel shows that any type of addiction, even one as innocuous as romantic feelings for another, often results in an individual feeling powerless and futile. "Queer" is a short, easy read, and a pretty good introduction to Burroughs' if one hasn't read any of his work before. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10624914050221787602" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <span id="freeText14568056039600737961" style="display:none">I enjoy a book about a hot mess, So I was more than a little excited when I came across this beauty at the thrift store. <div id="review_2645976704" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2645976704" />
    <a title="Kartika" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/39999117-kartika"><img alt="Kartika" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528037641p2/39999117.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2645976704?book_show_action=true">Jan 04, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kartika" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kartika" href="/user/show/39999117-kartika">Kartika</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2645976704" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17026777746617497690">Rating: 4⭐/5<br/><br/>William S. Burroughs wrote Queer in the early 1950s. Meant to be written as part two of his debut novel Junkie‚ Queer followed a completely different narrative. Queer is written in a third person narrative throughout the entire story except for the fact that it changes into a first person narrative in epilogue and it reads very naturally and isn’t at all confusing.<br/><br/>The need for the change of narrative by William S. Burroughs was a matter of heated debate between the author and the edi</span>
  <span id="freeText17026777746617497690" style="display:none">Rating: 4⭐/5<br /><br />William S. Burroughs wrote Queer in the early 1950s. Meant to be written as part two of his debut novel Junkie‚ Queer followed a completely different narrative. Queer is written in a third person narrative throughout the entire story except for the fact that it changes into a first person narrative in epilogue and it reads very naturally and isn’t at all confusing.<br /><br />The need for the change of narrative by William S. Burroughs was a matter of heated debate between the author and the editor. Burroughs believed that the change is necessary since the fragmented and disintegrated Lee required an observer to recite his story whereas in the epilogue: Mexico City return‚ lack of Allerton can only be explained by Lee himself in his first person narrative. When Burroughs was confronted on the matter‚ he merely argued saying something along the lines of “So what if it has not been done before. Why can’t a writer change narrative in the middle of a book? Screw it. I will write it this way.”<br /><br />Queer is a semi-autobiographical story of William S. Burroughs‚ similar to Junkie‚ yet vastly different. Junkie explored the ideas and experiences of Lee (alter ego of Burroughs) as a heroin addict whereas Queer is in honor of his desire‚ his queerness and overt sexual need that stems from Lee’s severe withdrawal from the drug. But towards the end of the book‚ one clearly understands that‚ Lee’s needs go beyond his sexual need and his desire for boys. It goes beyond a bodily connection. He craves for contact‚ a contact that doesn’t require touching or talking but merely requires feeling. His hunger for this feeling is not fulfilled or satisfied by Allerton‚ credits to his distance. The hurt cuts deep and stays behind with Lee till the end.<br /><br />Allerton was actually Adelbert Lewis Marker‚ an American Navy serviceman whom Burroughs befriended in Mexico City. There was something about Lee that bothered Allerton‚ he couldn’t quite put a finger on it. But in my opinion‚ the mask that Lee put on for Allerton‚ the monologues that came to him like dictation‚ routine he called them‚ was the reason Allerton felt something odd and crazy about that man. Lee’s routine was again a result of his drug withdrawal. He needed an audience and Allerton proved to be the calm and perfect audience for the same. Allerton realized this as well.<br /><br />This book itself was intended for just one person. This book‚ unlike Junkie‚ asked to be written and it is a passionate book about human desire. Kerouac‚ who lived with Burroughs for a while‚ describes Burroughs in the process of writing Queer as a frenzied calm man‚ a man with dual sides to him.<br /><br />I picked this book up solely because of how intriguing Burroughs’ life sounded to me‚ albeit tragic. He was openly bisexual in his relationship with Joan‚ and he loved her. Both of them were addicted to Benzedrine and Burroughs was an added heroin addict. His bisexuality never threatened his relationship with Joan. However‚ coupled with his addiction‚ Burroughs started to sleep with men excessively and this concerned Joan. On one day in September 1951‚ Burroughs asked Joan to play William-tell with him at the café in front of all their friends and acquaintances. They weren’t in their right mindsets or level of sanity to judge their actions themselves. An accidental shot that missed the glass on Joan’s head went straight through her and killed her. This is metaphorically described in a scene in Queer where Lee puts on a show in Ship Ahoy with a busboy and a mouse. The busboy holds the mouse by its tail with its head hanging and Lee shoots it right in its head and kills it saying – “If you’d get any closer the mouse would have clogged the muzzle.”<br /><br />Queer was written in the early 1950s‚ as I said‚ but it was only published three decades later‚ in 1985. The reasons for such a delay are few in number. When the manuscript was complete and sent to the editor for review‚ it was called to be too short and uninteresting. It was also told to Burroughs that if he publishes this he would get in trouble and would end up in jail. Soon‚ in the late 1950s and early 1960s‚ a publisher came forward to publish Queer but Burroughs denied the offer and called the work amateurish. But the absence of Queer was filled by his work “The naked lunch” which also talks about Joan’s death and his remorse and how the accident affected his life. But Queer has a substance that is in itself strange‚ absurd and chaotic which fits well with Burroughs’ then mindset.<br /><br />After Joan’s death‚ Burroughs left Mexico to escape sentence. He left for South America and was asked to continue his work as a writer. The book Junkie ends on the note of Lee planning to set out to look for the drug Yage‚ a drug that would allow telepathy. In Queer‚ Lee sets out with Allerton to South America‚ in search for Yage. He doesn’t succeed in his quest and loses contact with Allerton. I see it as a metaphor for his end of drug addiction and withdrawal. <br /><br />The epilogue‚ titled as Mexico City return‚ corresponds with his arrival in Mexico after Joan’s death. “I flew up to México city. I was a little nervous going through the airport: some cop or immigration inspector might spot me.” <br />The lines “In 1949 it was a cheap place to live….a single man could live well there for two dollars a day” in Introduction written by Burroughs gives us an idea about then Mexico. The scene soon changes in epilogue where the tourist with Lee talks about how he was asked to pay the taxi driver two dollars for dropping him at the airport. The timeline change is evident and so is the change of society. The book also talks about how queer people were looked at in Mexico City back in those times‚ either they didn’t criticize at all as it was not in their nature to accept or give criticism or they yelled crude words at people passing by.<br /><br />The imagery described in Queer is astounding and very strange. It is almost otherworldly to read some of the descriptions‚ especially those that described Lee’s desire for Allerton.<br />The usage of the word fag instead of homosexual is to represent the outlook of the society towards homosexuals at that time and the crude language is shown as a part of it.<br /><br />The book had a lot of sexist remarks that annoyed me a little bit. i don't think I should expect much from male authors of then. And warning: pedophilic content.<br />Overall‚ I loved reading this book and I highly recommend it. I haven’t read Junkie yet and it isn’t really a bad choice to read Queer first. I think they are completely different books and can be read separately.<br /><br />Thanks to Storytel for providing me with an audiobook of the same.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17026777746617497690" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_120044046" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/120044046" />
    <a title="Matt Piechocinski" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1007776-matt-piechocinski"><img alt="Matt Piechocinski" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1523916092p2/1007776.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/120044046?book_show_action=true">Sep 04, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt Piechocinski" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt Piechocinski" href="/user/show/1007776-matt-piechocinski">Matt Piechocinski</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer120044046" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1923075374799516382">I think the title of this book is a bit of a misnomer, and it appeals to everyone, regardless of sexuality. Why? Because everyone has been in the situation where they find themselves pining over, or maybe even loving someone, who doesn't reciprocate emotionally. The fact that Burroughs is gay, is irrelevent, because the hurt and sadness is real, and everyone has felt it. I found myself really identifying with Lee in this way, more so than I could in Junkie ... and Allerton read like a Bret Easto</span>
  <span id="freeText1923075374799516382" style="display:none">I think the title of this book is a bit of a misnomer, and it appeals to everyone, regardless of sexuality. Why? Because everyone has been in the situation where they find themselves pining over, or maybe even loving someone, who doesn't reciprocate emotionally. The fact that Burroughs is gay, is irrelevent, because the hurt and sadness is real, and everyone has felt it. I found myself really identifying with Lee in this way, more so than I could in Junkie ... and Allerton read like a Bret Easton Ellis character, but really representative of the type of guy my gay friends complain about on the dating scene ... shallow, vapid, and only into himself. Good book.</span>

There was something beautiful about their relationship and the end made me sad. It was these two together that made this novel for me. Am</span>

<div id="review_305373559" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/305373559" />
    <a title="Kyle Shroufe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6152481-kyle-shroufe"><img alt="Kyle Shroufe" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/305373559?book_show_action=true">Apr 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kyle Shroufe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kyle Shroufe" href="/user/show/6152481-kyle-shroufe">Kyle Shroufe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6152481-kyle-shroufe?shelf=read-beat-lost">read-beat-lost</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer305373559" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer743484910956500874">A William S. Burroughs novel that had been written just after his controversial novel "Junky" except it never got to see the light of day until the 1980's when, frankly, it was safe to publish it (and Burroughs finished the text). The idea that Burroughs even wrote this account of a homo-sexual relationship that spans continents and also inhabits heavy drug use and other topics that would be very played upon for our white picket fence society of post war America in late 40's early 50's, is just </span>
  <span id="freeText743484910956500874" style="display:none">A William S. Burroughs novel that had been written just after his controversial novel "Junky" except it never got to see the light of day until the 1980's when, frankly, it was safe to publish it (and Burroughs finished the text). The idea that Burroughs even wrote this account of a homo-sexual relationship that spans continents and also inhabits heavy drug use and other topics that would be very played upon for our white picket fence society of post war America in late 40's early 50's, is just another amazing thing to be said about the author and the "Beat Generation" in general. No other literary movement of this century has pushed against the mainstream and pushed against censorship than the 'Beats' and I think it is books like these and obviously others like Allen Ginbergs "Howl" that helped push censorship where it should be and keep it out of our literature. Literature is art and there should be no censorship in true art. Especially not art as great as this. I actually found "Queer" to be more fulfilling as a novella than "Junky" was as a novel. It's a short brisk read but it is bulky in content and continues the story of Lee (protagonist of "Junky" and "The Yage Letters") and is very well written. By far one of my favorite Burroughs books, it is a reflection on his own experiances in life (Burroughs was a homosexual man) and the fact that he pulls no punches makes his writing even more full fledged and less held back. The extreme pain in his writing can be debilitating as a reader, there are times you have to fight through the book (in a good way) but the fight is worth it because the payoff is extensive. This novel is a piece of work meant, along with others, to be read more than once. In all reality thats the only way to understand completley Burroughs point and more importantly his pain and struggle through life. "Queer" is a must read for any Beat, Lost Generation, or Post War Modernist fans and needs to be read for literature's sake. 5 out of 5 stars, if you read it once and don't like it try to read some of Burroughs other work and come back to "Queer" later on, although I will be honest queer is about as simple and straight forward as a William S. Burroughs novel comes. That's why I love him as an author.</span>
  <a data-text-id="743484910956500874" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_124634041" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/124634041" />
    <a title="John David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4357283-john-david"><img alt="John David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1581440570p2/4357283.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/124634041?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John David" href="/user/show/4357283-john-david">John David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140083897" href="/book/show/773072.Queer">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4357283-john-david?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer124634041" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12731519582444040956">This book has been sitting on my library shelves for a couple of years untouched. Since it was William Burroughs, and looked like a fairly quick read, I decided to pick it up. Burroughs is one of the seminal American authors of the underground gay experience, right? I thought it would be like reading Alan Hollinghurst on cocaine - something I was looking forward to.<br/><br/>But I was highly disappointed. The novel's plot revolves around gay two heroin addicts, William Lee and Eugene Allerton. Lee's attra</span>
  <span id="freeText12731519582444040956" style="display:none">This book has been sitting on my library shelves for a couple of years untouched. Since it was William Burroughs, and looked like a fairly quick read, I decided to pick it up. Burroughs is one of the seminal American authors of the underground gay experience, right? I thought it would be like reading Alan Hollinghurst on cocaine - something I was looking forward to.<br /><br />But I was highly disappointed. The novel's plot revolves around gay two heroin addicts, William Lee and Eugene Allerton. Lee's attraction to Allerton is completely and painfully unreciprocated. Despite all of Lee's attempts (which come in the form of embarrassing barside disquisitions in Mexican cantinas) to win Allerton's affections, it is all for naught. They decide to travel in search of some hallucinogenic drug which can only be obtained in the remote rainforest, and Lee promises to pay Allerton's way if he has sex with him a couple of times a week. In the end, the reader gets the impression that the quest for the drug is upset, much like Lee's wish for Allerton to love and appreciate him. The structure of the novel seems unmotivated and disinterested. It really seems to have no narrative "drive." I'm certainly not a reader that needs an action-packed novel by any stretch of the imagination, but there is nothing that compels the reader to keep reading - not even a chance of catching the two characters in licentious acts.<br /><br />But for anyone out there that wants to discover Burroughs for themselves, I definitely recommend this as a first step: it is eminently readable, unlike some of Burroughs' later, more experimental fiction. For this reason, it is a perfect choice for readers who have not hitherto been introduced to some of the more difficult aspects of twentieth century fiction, like non-linear narration, that symptom of dread postmodernism. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12731519582444040956" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_653620440" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/653620440" />
    <a title="Guy Portman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11566536-guy-portman"><img alt="Guy Portman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539458477p2/11566536.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/653620440?book_show_action=true">Jun 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Guy Portman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Guy Portman" href="/user/show/11566536-guy-portman">Guy Portman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer653620440" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16061065569478592990">Queer is an unreciprocated love story, in which the protagonist Lee craves love and attention from a young American by the name of Eugene Allerton. <br/><br/>Set in the American ex-pat scene of hedonistic, lawless 1940s Mexico, the first half of the story centres around a number of bars and gay joints where Lee spends his days, drinking, drug-taking and going through set routines, whereby he attempts to regale his audience with intellectualness and bizarre humour. <br/><br/>Later in the book Lee persuades Allerton </span>
  <span id="freeText16061065569478592990" style="display:none">Queer is an unreciprocated love story, in which the protagonist Lee craves love and attention from a young American by the name of Eugene Allerton. <br /><br />Set in the American ex-pat scene of hedonistic, lawless 1940s Mexico, the first half of the story centres around a number of bars and gay joints where Lee spends his days, drinking, drug-taking and going through set routines, whereby he attempts to regale his audience with intellectualness and bizarre humour. <br /><br />Later in the book Lee persuades Allerton with the aid of a financial incentive to join him on a journey to South America in search of yage, an elusive drug purported to have telepathic qualities. The trip finds the sensitive Lee clasping at rare moments of tenderness, suffering from opiate sickness and longing for young boys observed along the way. <br /><br />Autobiographical in nature, the book is an account of Burroughs’s life in Mexico, during a troubled time in his life shortly after accidentally shooting wife Joan Vollmer dead. Self-deprecating throughout, Burroughs adeptly portrays a deep sense of longing and loss, oftentimes amplified by withdrawal and junk sickness. Though Queer’s subject matter was extremely controversial at the time it was written, the homosexual encounters are not delved into in great detail and the descriptions are not as explicit and lurid as in some of the author’s other books. <br /><br />Queer is an excellent introduction to Burroughs’s writing, in part because it is more coherent than much of his later experimental work, such as The Naked Lunch. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16061065569478592990" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1400743550?book_show_action=true">Sep 25, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason" href="/user/show/12831990-jason">Jason</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12831990-jason?shelf=own">own</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1400743550" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16751777833577878756">Desperation and self-loathing.<br/><br/>With much of the unapologetic voyeuristic view as was in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23940.Junky' title='Junky by William S. Burroughs' rel='nofollow noopener'>Junky</a>, but less rawness in a way. Lee, the protagonist is painful to be with, you cringe and want to distance yourself. It seems that the character's view of the outside world is a reflection of his inward one - disgusting, empty, meaninglessness. A painful book, hard to reckon with, and unfinished in my view; I do not need a clear resolution, but it just seemed like this was a piece, a fragment, and it could </span>
  <span id="freeText16751777833577878756" style="display:none">Desperation and self-loathing.<br /><br />With much of the unapologetic voyeuristic view as was in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23940.Junky" title="Junky by William S. Burroughs" rel="nofollow noopener">Junky</a>, but less rawness in a way. Lee, the protagonist is painful to be with, you cringe and want to distance yourself. It seems that the character's view of the outside world is a reflection of his inward one - disgusting, empty, meaninglessness. A painful book, hard to reckon with, and unfinished in my view; I do not need a clear resolution, but it just seemed like this was a piece, a fragment, and it could have been something much more. I feel like I just read an excerpt that is essentially a glimpse at some self-imposed outer/minor circle of hell for this singular gay man. <br /><br />Lee lusts after Allerton, a young unaffected man that doesn't seem to reciprocate Lee's feelings, but that doesn't stop Lee from persisting in his obsession. Lee keeps setting himself up for further pointless degradation, while wishing he could force others to his will. The reader may feel sorry for this leering, clearly clever, masochist at times, but it is hard to really sympathize, nor does Allerton inspire any kindly feelings. <br /><br />Tough one to get through even with it's short page count. Burroughs does rough and ugly well, I just had a harder time getting much out of this one. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16751777833577878756" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/215219087?book_show_action=true">Sep 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jaded" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jaded" href="/user/show/1065366-jaded">Jaded</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1065366-jaded?shelf=adult-themed">adult-themed</a>,

          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer215219087" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2108063969542455311">This is an interesting look into the heart and hormones of William S. Burroughs. In many ways it is quite sad how being a member of a counter-culture group back in an era where the homophobia and persecution of those who were not WASP's was violent and deadly stunted the expression and romantic feelings of a gay man. It is equally as sad, how little this nation has changed with his hatred and judgement of people.<br/><br/>You come away with a feeling of bleak sadness for Burroughs that all of the love he </span>
  <span id="freeText2108063969542455311" style="display:none">This is an interesting look into the heart and hormones of William S. Burroughs. In many ways it is quite sad how being a member of a counter-culture group back in an era where the homophobia and persecution of those who were not WASP's was violent and deadly stunted the expression and romantic feelings of a gay man. It is equally as sad, how little this nation has changed with his hatred and judgement of people.<br /><br />You come away with a feeling of bleak sadness for Burroughs that all of the love he seeks is riddled with snares and barbs and twisted lust fueled by his addictions to drugs and alcohol. True love is tough to find and live with as it is, let alone trying to love and fight societal prejudice, urgings of the groin, addiction, and being an obtuse artist.<br /><br />This book isn't for everyone, so use your best judgement if you chose to read it. Don't pick it up to "figure out" the queer mindset or lifestyle, because you would be wasting your time. If you want to read what goes through the heart and mind of a troubled man, then this is your book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2108063969542455311" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1479042750" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1479042750" />
    <a title="Herdis Marie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19823181-herdis-marie"><img alt="Herdis Marie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1521725491p2/19823181.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1479042750?book_show_action=true">Dec 25, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Herdis Marie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Herdis Marie" href="/user/show/19823181-herdis-marie">Herdis Marie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141189918" href="/book/show/10287367-queer">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19823181-herdis-marie?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19823181-herdis-marie?shelf=american">american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19823181-herdis-marie?shelf=lgbtqia">lgbtqia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1479042750" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18061916727073404382">I really wanted to like this.<br/><br/>I really did.<br/><br/>But I realise that my problem with a lot of modernist and postmodernist literature is its propensity for nonsense. I get that it's supposed to mirror a modern world that thrives on chaos, that the lack of clear plot lines and characterisation are a mark of the modern world's tendency towards confusion with our very state of being, blah, blah, blah. But me, I really like a character I can get properly into. I don't even have to like him/her (my favourite </span>
  <span id="freeText18061916727073404382" style="display:none">I really wanted to like this.<br /><br />I really did.<br /><br />But I realise that my problem with a lot of modernist and postmodernist literature is its propensity for nonsense. I get that it's supposed to mirror a modern world that thrives on chaos, that the lack of clear plot lines and characterisation are a mark of the modern world's tendency towards confusion with our very state of being, blah, blah, blah. But me, I really like a character I can get properly into. I don't even have to like him/her (my favourite book is "The Picture of Dorian Gray" and its protagonist is a complete douche). But I have to be engaged by the characters, otherwise my attention just drifts and eventually fades. Hence, it took me several days to get through a book I would normally have managed in a few hours.<br /><br />The setting is interesting, the descriptions are vivid and sometimes even riveting, but the book follows a character with whom it is (in my opinion) completely impossible to sympathise, mainly because it seems that he is entirely lacking in any kind of logical motivation for any of his actions. I mean, I get that he's horny as fuck. But come on.<br /><br />Anyway, this was my first foray into Burroughs' work, and it might simply be that his writing just isn't my particular cup of tea.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18061916727073404382" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('B0dxGulpQN0dEg9lKXbo8XoB84KDbnVuvYibMKxB84g9YRjIUjeJtbo5uwTeg+IurF4USk3j+wnm9t2BYQIBEg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('WkDFLVJE8d/qKAzrX8ELf+G2Dn4RLCl5uc/S7hZcLN9gZqz/6Ro4t00DuIqoNAGgN+nptt+hpx7isZRf2x/eRQ==')})
  }
